##### WorldGen & Biomes  [key=worldgen-biomes]
SUMMARY: The generation pipeline (Perlin -> Whittaker classify -> freeze to integer grid) is architecturally sound and its determinism contract is genuinely well-built and well-tested. The biome-degradation math (lazy spatial field, integer-exact catch-up, implicit latch, asymmetric step-penalty) is sophisticated and the math-only tests pass. However two real problems dominate: (1) the shipped DegradePeriod=100 contradicts the doc, the code comments, and the acceptance tests (all say 10), currently fails 12 M9 tests, and effectively re-opens the "extract forever from one tile" problem M9 existed to close; and (2) StartPicker's first-match (y,x) scan jams the castle into the map's top/left corner on nearly every seed and makes no attempt to balance resource distance, so generated starts are neither central nor fair. Multi-player fair placement is entirely unimplemented despite the conquest design.
STRENGTHS:
  + Freeze-the-grid determinism is correctly designed and proven by tests :: MapGenerator runs the float Perlin/Whittaker pipeline ONCE and freezes an integer Biome[,] before the sim sees it (MapGenerator.cs:8-29, world-generation.md:11-16), keeping all float math off the replay path. WorldGenTests.TwinRun_OnGeneratedGenesis_HashesMatch and Snapshot_RoundTrips_OnGeneratedWorld actually pin that the generator's floats never leak into sim state -- these pass. This is exactly the right boundary for a deterministic discrete-event engine.
  + Water = passable-but-expensive (cost 250) elegantly kills a whole bug class :: Instead of Impassable water that can trap the castle or make tiles unreachable, Biomes.MoveCost(Water)=250 (Biome.cs:26-35) keeps every tile reachable while still routing armies around lakes, and EveryTile_IsReachableFromStart flood-tests it. A one-line design choice that removes the entire 'disconnected pocket / null path' family of generation failures (world-generation.md:26-35).
  + Biome degradation math is sophisticated and observation-independent :: The lazy spatial-field model (BiomeDegradation.cs) does integer-exact, remainder-carrying catch-up with an IMPLICIT desert latch (no stored flag -- derived from baseline+deviation < threshold) and a constant-rate-between-transitions invariant. The math-only acceptance tests (BiomeFertilityCatchUpTests) all pass, including the observation-independence and snap tests. It honours the engine's no-global-tick rule while still modelling spreading land exhaustion.
  + Asymmetric step-penalty and MAX-not-sum are genuinely good anti-exploit design :: Downward band crossings snap fertility to the next band's baseline (Forest->Grassland to 50, not 74) while recovery climbs smoothly (BiomeDegradation.cs:290-349, biome-degradation.md:75-114), so a biome flip is a durable loss the player can't duty-cycle out of. And overlapping extractors take MAX of degrade amounts, not SUM (BiomeDegradation.cs:197-213), so you can't park N camps to instantly desertify a strip. Both are the right primitives -- they would work well if the DegradePeriod constant were correct.
FINDINGS (kept=7, refuted/dropped=0):

  [CRITICAL] (logic-hole | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=critical) DegradePeriod=100 contradicts doc/comments/tests, breaks 12 M9 tests, and disables biome degradation in practice
    EVIDENCE: BiomeDegradationConfig.cs:70-72 sets `DegradePeriod: 100` directly under the comment "10 ticks aligns with ProductionPeriodTicks (LumberCamp=10, Farm=10) so degrade and production tick at the same cadence." Every other artifact says 10: biome-degradation.md:197 ("a single global BiomeDegradationConfig.DegradePeriod (10 ticks)"), StructureCatalog.cs:99-108 Farm comment ("1 fertility per DegradePeriod (10 ticks) ... ~260 ticks") and :40-43 LumberCamp ("~1000 ticks"), and BiomeDegradationTests.cs:19 uses `Cfg = new()` (the default, =100) while asserting 10-tick behavior (line 72-76 expects own tile Grassland at t=260; line 137 expects fertility 26 at t=500). I ran the suite: 12 of 57 tests FAIL, including the keystone `LumberCamp_OnForest_DegradesOwnTile_ThenStops_TheHeadline` and `Farm_OnGrassland_DegradesToDesert_PermanentlyDead`. At period 100, a Forest tile (ForestBaseline 100, ForestThreshold 75, DegradeAmount 1) takes 26*100=2600 ticks to flip to Grassland vs the intended 260. Worse, degrade (1 per 100) is now SLOWER than recovery (RecoveryAmount 1 / RecoveryPeriod 30 = 1 per 30, config lines 66-67): a LumberCamp fills its 30 buffer in ~150 ticks then goes dormant; while dormant the tile recovers ~3x faster than it degraded, so a normally-hauled/duty-cycled camp never crosses a band threshold.
    IMPACT:   The entire point of M9 (biome-degradation.md, '"This is what ends extract-forever from one tile"') is defeated. With the shipped constant a player parks one LumberCamp/Farm and harvests a single tile essentially forever because the land recovers between buffer-fill cycles faster than it degrades. The strategic 'move on / manage the land' pressure that the whole milestone, the step-penalty, and the latch were built to create simply does not fire on any realistic timescale, and the acceptance tests that encode the intended behavior are red.
    FIX:      Set `DegradePeriod` back to 10 to match the comment, the design doc, the catalog timings, and the acceptance tests (core-touching: BiomeDegradationConfig.cs is in Sim.Core). If 100 was a deliberate softening, that decision is self-inconsistent (it breaks the 'same cadence as production' goal and inverts degrade<->recovery ordering) and must instead be expressed by lowering DegradeAmount or raising RecoveryPeriod while keeping degrade faster than recovery, and the doc/comments/tests updated. Either way, get the 12 tests green again.
    VERIFY:   Verified directly. BiomeDegradationConfig.cs:72 ships `DegradePeriod: 100`, sitting under the SAME code block's comment (lines 70-72) saying '10 ticks aligns with ProductionPeriodTicks' â€” an internal contradiction that is a typo signature, not an intentional retune. Every other artifact says 10: biome-degradation.md:196 ('a single global BiomeDegradationConfig.DegradePeriod (10 ticks)'), StructureCatalog.cs:42 ('~1000 ticks') and :99-107 ('1 fertility per DegradePeriod (10 ticks) ... ~260 ticks'), and the test math throughout BiomeDegradationTests.cs. I RAN the suite: `dotnet test --filter BiomeDegradationTests` returns Failed: 12, Passed: 6, Total: 18 â€” the exact 12 named, including the keystone LumberCamp_OnForest_DegradesOwnTile_ThenStops_TheHeadline and Farm_OnGrassland_DegradesToDesert_PermanentlyDead. The recovery inversion is real: degrade = 1/100 (0.010/t) is slower than recovery = RecoveryAmount 1 / RecoveryPeriod 30 (0.033/t, config lines 66-67) via DeriveRate (BiomeDegradation.cs:179,183), so a duty-cycled camp recovers ~3.3x faster than it degrades and never crosses a band threshold. The M9 'ends extract-forever' purpose is defeated. Critical stands.

  [HIGH] (balance | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=high) StartPicker guarantees resource-biome existence within radius 15 but not proximity, so haul distances vary ~2x across seeds
    EVIDENCE: StartPicker only tests `HasNearby(... radius ...)` for biome EXISTENCE (StartPicker.cs:27-29, 39-55) with radius 15 (GenerationConfig.cs:48). It never compares or minimizes distance. Measured nearest-Mountain Chebyshev distance across the same 12 seeds: 15,15,15,7,13,8,15,15,7,15,15,15 -> 7 of 12 seeds have the only reachable Mountain at exactly the radius limit (15), while seeds 4/6/9 have it at 7-8. Because the scan takes the FIRST qualifying tile and high-elevation Mountain is rarely adjacent to the top-edge Grassland it picks, it systematically biases toward maximal mountain distance. Mountain share itself drops to ~1.5-2.2% on some seeds (8,2,11,12), so Stone can be both scarce and far.
    IMPACT:   Stone (Quarry/Mountain) is required to build Mines (StructureCatalog.cs:75-78) and Towers, so a start with Mountain at distance 15 vs 7 roughly doubles the stone-haul round-trip and meaningfully slows the whole tech/expansion curve. Two seeds that both 'pass' the picker can hand players very different economies -- the opposite of fair-while-asleep. The metric the picker optimizes (existence) is not the metric that matters (haul cost).
    FIX:      Have the picker score candidates by summed/maximal distance to the four resource biomes and pick the best (lowest) rather than the first qualifying tile, or tighten the per-biome radius (e.g. Mountain within 8). This pairs naturally with the center-out scan fix. Core-touching (StartPicker.cs / GenerationConfig.cs).
    VERIFY:   Verified. StartPicker.HasNearby (StartPicker.cs:39-55) tests biome EXISTENCE only; nothing minimizes distance, and WorldGenTests.cs:65-67 asserts only existence-within-radius, so no test guards proximity. I reproduced the auditor's nearest-Mountain Chebyshev vector EXACTLY at radius 15: 15,15,15,7,13,8,15,15,7,15,15,15 (7/12 pinned at the cap), and confirmed low Mountain shares (seed 8=1.5%, 2=2.1%, 11=2.2%, 12=2.0%). The '~2x' is if anything understated: at the production radius 64 on a 256-map the haul variance widens toward ~10x. Forest/Grassland (early-critical Wood/Food) are always adjacent (distF=1 in most seeds) so the start is never crippled, but Stone gates Mines (StructureCatalog.cs:75-78) and Towers, and the topmost-Grassland pick structurally sits farthest from interior mountains. For a 'fair-while-asleep' game this is a genuine high-severity gap. Confirmed.

  [HIGH] (balance | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=high) StartPicker first-match (y,x) scan jams the castle into the map's top/left corner on nearly every seed
    EVIDENCE: StartPicker.Pick (StartPicker.cs:22-31) scans y outer, x inner and returns the FIRST Grassland tile that has Forest+Hills+Mountain within Chebyshev radius (GenerationConfig.cs:48, StartSearchRadius=15). I generated 12 maps (64x64, seeds 1-12) against the built Sim.Core: starts were (6,0),(29,0),(51,1),(0,0),(34,13),(0,0),(0,0),(0,21),(41,0),(48,0),(0,0),(0,43). 4 of 12 land on exactly (0,0); 11 of 12 sit on an edge (x=0 or y in {0,1}); only seed 5 (34,13) is interior. The (y,x) scan provably returns the topmost-then-leftmost qualifying tile, so the castle is structurally pinned to the boundary.
    IMPACT:   A boundary start means ~half of the castle's surrounding area is off-map, so the player's expansion/defense room and the explorable world are halved, the castle is maximally exposed on a map edge, and every game opens in the same cramped corner regardless of seed -- the procedural variety is largely cosmetic. For the host's actual 2-faction setup this is compounded: both castles cluster near one corner (see multiplayer finding), wasting most of the generated continent.
    FIX:      Replace the top-left linear scan with an equally-deterministic, auditable center-out scan (spiral or distance-from-center ordering) so the castle lands near the playable interior. The design doc's stated reason for avoiding RNG (determinism/auditability) is fully preserved by a center-out ordering; no randomness needed. Core-touching (StartPicker.cs).
    VERIFY:   Verified by re-reading StartPicker.cs:22-31 (y-outer/x-inner scan, returns FIRST qualifying Grassland) and re-running generation against the built Sim.Core. At default radius 15 I reproduced the auditor's coordinate sequence EXACTLY: (6,0),(29,0),(51,1),(0,0),(34,13),(0,0),(0,0),(0,21),(41,0),(48,0),(0,0),(0,43) â€” 4/12 at exactly (0,0). Crucially the auditor measured at radius 15, but the REAL server (WorldFactory.cs:29) uses StartSearchRadius = max(28, max(w,h)/4) on a 256x256 default map (ServerOptions.cs:10-11). At that production config (radius 64) the bias is WORSE: for seeds 100-111 I got 12/12 on the top edge and 10/12 at exactly (0,0). The (y,x) first-match scan provably returns the topmost-then-leftmost qualifier; on a varied map that is essentially always the boundary. High confirmed (arguably understated at the shipped map size).

  [MEDIUM] (logic-hole | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=medium) A seed with no qualifying start throws and crashes the host; no retry/relax path exists
    EVIDENCE: MapGenerator.Build throws InvalidOperationException when StartPicker returns null (MapGenerator.cs:23-26). The doc says the caller 'decides (retry with different seed, relax thresholds)' (StartPicker.cs:11-12, world-generation.md:39-44), but the only production caller, WorldFactory.Build (WorldFactory.cs:31), calls `MapGenerator.Build(cfg)` with no try/catch and no retry. StartPicker requires Forest AND Hills AND Mountain all within radius (StartPicker.cs:27-29), so a flat/low-mountain seed (Mountain share already dips to ~1.5%) or a smaller map can legitimately yield no candidate.
    IMPACT:   Once 'share a map seed' / player-chosen seeds become reachable (world-generation.md:63 says it's already plumbed through GeneratedMap.Seed), a single bad seed hard-crashes the server with an unhandled exception instead of degrading gracefully. It is a latent availability bug sitting directly on the player-facing seed path.
    FIX:      In WorldFactory, wrap Build in a bounded retry that perturbs the seed (or progressively relaxes StartSearchRadius / drops the Mountain requirement) and surfaces a clean error only after exhausting attempts. The generator stays pure; the retry policy lives in the host.
    VERIFY:   Verified, and reachable TODAY rather than purely latent. MapGenerator.cs:23-26 throws InvalidOperationException on null start; the sole production caller WorldFactory.cs:31 has no try/catch/retry despite StartPicker.cs:11-12 instructing the caller to retry/relax. The picker requires Forest AND Hills AND Mountain ALL in radius (StartPicker.cs:27-29). ServerOptions.Parse (ServerOptions.cs:14-37) already exposes --mapseed/--width/--height, so an operator controls the inputs now. Searching small maps under the server radius rule, 168/200 configs threw and EVERY 12x12 seed crashed ('No valid start found in 12x12 map with seed 1'). The realistic 256x256 default never crashed in my runs, which is why medium is right â€” a normal operator won't hit it unless they pick a small/flat map, but when they do the host dies with an unhandled exception and no graceful degradation. Medium confirmed.

  [MEDIUM] (missing-feature | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=medium) Multi-player fair start placement is unimplemented; host places the 2nd faction resource-blind and only 12 tiles away
    EVIDENCE: StartPicker exposes only `Pick` -> a single TileCoord (StartPicker.cs:18); there is no PickMany / fairness-distance API (world-generation.md:61 lists it as future work). The real server (WorldFactory.cs:88-98, 114-126) places faction 1 at the nearest Grassland found by `FindGrasslandNear(map, start, 12)` -- a plain Grassland scan with NO Forest/Hills/Mountain guarantee and a fixed ~12-Chebyshev distance from player 0. Only player 0's start gets the resource-biome guarantee.
    IMPACT:   The design vision is a multi-player conquest game ('conquer every enemy castle') played asynchronously on geological time with telegraphed war. Both pillars are violated here: the enemy castle can be dropped on Grassland surrounded by water/desert with no Mountain or Forest in reach (a resource-starved, unwinnable start), and at ~12 grassland tiles (~120 move cost) it is far too close for an async, usually-offline game where war is supposed to be slow and telegraphed. The fairness the design promises for combat does not exist in generation.
    FIX:      Implement `StartPicker.PickMany(grid, n, minSeparation)` that returns N tiles each satisfying the resource-proximity check AND a minimum mutual Chebyshev distance scaled to map size, then have WorldFactory use it instead of FindGrasslandNear. Until then, at minimum run the existing single-tile resource check on the enemy tile so it isn't resource-starved.
    VERIFY:   Verified. StartPicker exposes only Pick -> single TileCoord (StartPicker.cs:18); no PickMany/fairness API. world-generation.md:61/67 defers multi-player fair start to 'with combat (M6)'. Combat IS implemented (Combat/Diplomacy/DeclareWar modules and their tests are present), so the deferral trigger is met yet the feature is absent â€” a real gap. WorldFactory.cs:89,114-126 places faction 1 via FindGrasslandNear(map,start,12): a plain Grassland ring scan, NO Forest/Hills/Mountain guarantee, fixed ~12 Chebyshev. Caveat keeping it at medium not high: the shipped 2nd faction is a fog/combat STUB (one Scout, 20 Wood, WorldFactory.cs:94-96), not a real opponent yet, so 'resource-starved unwinnable enemy start' is forward-looking; and the 'too close for async war' angle is weak since declare-war delay/notification apply regardless of distance. Missing-feature core is real. Medium confirmed.

  [LOW] (unnecessary | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=low) Hills/Mountain/Water fertility baselines are stored and snapshot-serialized but never affect anything
    EVIDENCE: BiomeDegradationConfig carries HillsBaseline 30, MountainBaseline 60, WaterBaseline 0 (BiomeDegradationConfig.cs:53-57), but IsOnLadder is true only for Forest/Grassland/Desert (BiomeDegradation.cs:55-56) and BiomeAt/FertilityAt return the worldgen biome/baseline for off-ladder tiles without ever banding by these values. The whole config struct is snapshot-serialized (GameWorld.cs:88, Snapshot), so three inert numbers ride in every save.
    IMPACT:   Minor over-engineering / dead config. It is explicitly justified as 'API uniformity' in the comment, so it is low-stakes, but it invites confusion (a reader may think tuning MountainBaseline does something) and is a small format/serialization cost for no behavior.
    FIX:      Either drop the off-ladder baselines from the struct (compute a single sentinel in BaselineFertility), or add a one-line assertion/comment at the read sites making it explicit they are never consulted. Lowest priority; safe to leave.
    VERIFY:   Verified. HillsBaseline 30 / MountainBaseline 60 / WaterBaseline 0 (BiomeDegradationConfig.cs:55-57) are returned by BaselineFertility (BiomeDegradation.cs:47-49), but IsOnLadder is true only for F/G/D (BiomeDegradation.cs:55-56) and both BiomeAt (line 92) and FertilityAt (line 80) short-circuit to the worldgen biome/baseline for off-ladder tiles without ever banding by these values. The config struct IS snapshot-serialized â€” Snapshot.cs:915-917 writes all three every save (format v6). The only live FertilityAt consumer of an off-ladder value would be host debug PrintRow (Program.cs:651-652), but that runs solely on extractor tiles (LumberCamp/Farm = ladder biomes), so even it never reads them. Gameplay-inert; the 'three inert ints per save' claim is exact. Minor over-engineering, justified in-comment as 'API uniformity'. Low confirmed.

  [LOW] (logic-hole | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=low) world-generation.md states Hills move cost 35 but code uses 25
    EVIDENCE: world-generation.md:50 reads 'MoveCost(Desert) = 40 -- slower than Hills (35), faster than Mountain (45)', but Biomes.MoveCost returns Hills => 25 (Biome.cs:26) and Mountain => 45 (Biome.cs:33). The relative ordering claim (Desert slower than Hills) still holds numerically (40>25), only the parenthetical 35 is wrong.
    IMPACT:   Pure documentation drift -- no gameplay impact today, but it misleads anyone tuning movement or balance from the doc, and Hills being 25 (cheaper than Forest's 30) is itself a non-obvious choice worth having documented correctly.
    FIX:      Update world-generation.md to cite the real Hills cost (25), or make the doc reference Biomes.MoveCost rather than restating numbers that can drift.
    VERIFY:   Verified verbatim. world-generation.md:50 reads 'MoveCost(Desert) = 40 -- slower than Hills (35), faster than Mountain (45)', but Biomes.MoveCost returns Hills => 25 (Biome.cs:26), Mountain => 45 (Biome.cs:27), Forest => 30 (Biome.cs:25). The parenthetical 35 is wrong; the ordering claim (Desert 40 > Hills) still holds, and Hills 25 being cheaper than Forest 30 is a real, undocumented choice. Pure doc drift, no gameplay impact. Low confirmed.

##### Movement & Pathfinding  [key=movement-pathfinding]
SUMMARY: The A* core is genuinely solid: deterministic, optimal, overflow-guarded, and the fog-aware plan/execute cost split is a load-bearing design that is well-reasoned and tested. The biggest problem is not in the algorithm but in authorization â€” MoveIntent never checks unit ownership, so a player can puppet enemy units (MoveGroupIntent does check, proving the omission is a bug). Secondary issues cluster around balance: the banded crowding curve saturates at 16 units while the occupancy cap is 50, so the design's stated anti-deathball lever is effectively disabled; the square 4-neighbour grid instantiates exactly the diagonal artifact Â§6.3 warned about; and per-tile tick costs sit in the same currency as population aging (TicksPerYear=250) in a way that makes foot journeys consume years of a unit's life on the default 256Ã—256 map. Several async-fairness edges (silent mid-march stop with no notice, no replan) and minor inconsistencies (foot units swim oceans; boats build roads on water) round out the list.
STRENGTHS:
  + A* is correct, optimal, and fully deterministic :: The open-set priority is the tuple (f, x, y) (Pathfinding.cs:29-55), which is a total order over tiles since (x,y) is unique and re-enqueues of the same tile always have strictly lower f â€” so dequeue order is deterministic without relying on .NET PriorityQueue stability. The Manhattan heuristic is consistent for the 4-neighbour topology and admissible against the MIN_COST=1 floor (correctly documented), giving optimal paths. The Impassable guard at Pathfinding.cs:48 prevents the int-overflow-wraps-to-negative-gScore infinite loop. Clean, well-commented, and the pure-read contract is enforced by RoadPathfindingTests.Pathfinding_IsPureRead_NoRoadMutation (100x hash-stable).
  + Fog-aware plan/execute cost split is a genuinely good design :: Splitting PlanCost (player-perspective, fog-gated, used by A*) from ExecutionCost (ground truth, used to schedule each hop) cleanly resolves the tension between 'don't let A* omnisciently route around unseen armies' and 'the unit must pay the real cost of where it steps' (MovementCost.cs:81-134, docs/movement-cost.md). The load-bearing test Path_DoesNotAvoidFoggedCrowd_RoutesStraightThrough pins the intended 'cost of ignorance' behavior. This is the kind of subtle invariant that's easy to get wrong and is both reasoned-out and tested here.
  + Committed-path storage protects determinism across snapshot/restore :: Storing PathRemaining on the unit and never recomputing it (MoveIntent.cs:84-134, Unit.cs:85-88) means a restored sim replays the exact route the live sim took, even though road conditions evolve â€” recomputing would risk a divergent path and a hash mismatch. This is the right call for a replay/durability-centric async game and is consistent with the road lazy-decay model.
  + Epoch fencing makes retask races robust :: AssignmentEpoch / MovementEpoch tokens captured at schedule time and checked on fire (MoveArrivalEvent.cs:33-37, Unit.cs:145-160) let a mid-flight move be safely diverted by a new MoveIntent without the old chain's arrivals corrupting position or scheduling continuations. MoveOnBusyTests exercises the nasty move-then-new-haul race and confirms stale events no-op. The explicit BumpEpoch for the Idleâ†’Idle move case shows real care about an easy-to-miss edge.
  + MIN_COST=1 floor doubles as a structural safety guarantee :: Because every passable tile costs at least 1 (RoadConstants.cs:14) and crowding only adds non-negative amounts, ExecutionCost is always >= 1, so each scheduled arrival is strictly in the future (sim.Now + cost). This quietly prevents 0-tick hops that would schedule an event at the current tick and risk same-tick re-entrancy / infinite arrival loops â€” a real hazard avoided almost for free.
FINDINGS (kept=9, refuted/dropped=0):

  [CRITICAL] (logic-hole | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=critical) MoveIntent does not verify unit ownership â€” a player can move enemy/neutral units
    EVIDENCE: MoveIntent.Resolve (src/Sim.Core/Movement/MoveIntent.cs:15-52) checks unit-exists, GroupId, IsEmbarked, breeding, and InBounds(Destination), but NEVER compares unit.OwnerId to the issuing PlayerId. By contrast MoveGroupIntent.Resolve (src/Sim.Core/Groups/MoveGroupIntent.cs:34-35) does exactly that: `if (group.OwnerId != PlayerId) return Reject(...)`. The Intent base even flags this as unfinished ('eventually used by validation when intents become player-scoped', src/Sim.Core/Intents/Intent.cs). IntentEvent.Apply just calls Intent.Resolve with no upstream gate (src/Sim.Core/Intents/IntentEvent.cs).
    IMPACT:   Any player can issue MoveIntent(enemyUnitId, anywhere) and it resolves as Applied. You can march an opponent's hauler away from its route, scatter their idle citizens, walk their lone units off to a corner, or pull a worker off an extractor (CleanUpAssignment runs on the victim's unit). In an asynchronous game whose entire premise is 'fair while asleep' and 'knowledge comes from presence', remote-controlling a sleeping opponent's economy is game-ending. The fact that the group variant guards ownership and the solo variant does not makes this an oversight, not a deliberate deferral.
    FIX:      core-touching: add `if (unit.OwnerId != PlayerId) return IntentOutcome.Reject(...)` at the top of MoveIntent.Resolve, mirroring MoveGroupIntent. Audit the rest of the solo-intent family (HaulIntent, AssignWorkers/Builders) for the same gap. Longer term, bind PlayerId to the authenticated connection in GameHost rather than trusting the client-supplied payload field.
    VERIFY:   Re-read MoveIntent.Resolve (MoveIntent.cs:15-52): it checks unit-exists, GroupId, IsEmbarked, breeding, InBounds(Destination) â€” and never compares unit.OwnerId to PlayerId, then calls CleanUpAssignment+BeginMove on the (possibly enemy) unit. Confirmed against the sibling pattern: a grep shows EVERY other unit/structure-targeting intent guards ownership â€” MoveGroupIntent.cs:34, FormGroupIntent.cs:44, DisbandGroupIntent.cs:27, TrainUnitIntent.cs:39/64, BeginBreedingIntent.cs:44/53, EmbarkIntent.cs:45/64, DisembarkIntent.cs:35. MoveIntent is the lone exception. HaulIntent.cs:32-81 ALSO lacks the check (so the hole is broader than reported â€” you can hijack an enemy hauler too). No test covers MoveIntent ownership rejection (MovementCrowdingTests/MoveOnBusy only use matching owner=0/player=0). Reachability is actually WORSE than the finding states: PlayerId is client-supplied (deserialized from payload at IntentJson.cs:69; GameHost.SubmitEnvelopeJson never overrides it from an authenticated session), and enemy unit Ids are exposed in the fog-filtered view (ViewProjector ToUnitDto), so an attacker uses their OWN honest PlayerId, targets a visible enemy UnitId, and IntentEvent.Apply (IntentEvent.cs:10) resolves it Applied â€” no identity forgery needed. Genuine critical logic hole; in an async 'fair-while-asleep' game, remote-controlling a sleeping opponent's units/economy is game-ending.

  [MEDIUM] (logic-hole | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=medium) Hard-cap rejection silently idles a unit mid-march with no player notification
    EVIDENCE: On cap overflow MoveArrivalEvent.Apply (src/Sim.Core/Movement/MoveArrivalEvent.cs:48-59) clears the path, sets Activity.Idle, and sets Outcome=Reject â€” but it is a ScheduledEvent, not an IntentEvent. GameHost.HarvestRejections only records notices for `log[i] is IntentEvent ie && ie.Outcome.IsRejected` (src/Sim.Server/GameHost.cs:117). So MoveArrivalEvent rejections produce no NoticeDto. The doc (docs/movement-cost.md 'Out of scope') confirms there is no wait-and-retry: 'the player re-issues.'
    IMPACT:   A hauler or scout whose committed path runs into a tile that is at cap (e.g. a transient enemy/own pileup that formed after planning, or a fog'd cap-saturated tile per the intended 'cost of ignorance' loop) stops dead on its previous tile, drops its itinerary, and goes Idle â€” and the offline player gets zero signal. In a game whose core promise is fairness while asleep, a logistics chain can quietly halt for hours with no way for the sleeping player to learn it happened. There is also no replan: even on fully-known terrain the unit cannot route around the blockage on its own.
    FIX:      Emit a player-facing notice on MoveArrivalEvent/GroupArrivalEvent cap rejection (extend HarvestRejections to attribute owner-scoped scheduled-event rejections, or schedule a dedicated notice event). Optionally add a bounded wait-and-retry (re-attempt the same hop after a delay) so a transient pileup doesn't permanently strand a caravan.
    VERIFY:   Confirmed. MoveArrivalEvent is a ScheduledEvent, not an IntentEvent (MoveArrivalEvent.cs:10); on cap overflow it clears the path, sets Idle, and sets Outcome=Reject (lines 48-59). GameHost.HarvestRejections only emits a NoticeDto for 'log[i] is IntentEvent ie && ie.Outcome.IsRejected' (GameHost.cs:117), so this rejection produces no notice â€” verified that HarvestRejections is the only NoticeDto producer in the server. There is also no replan/wait-retry: movement-cost.md 'Out of scope' confirms 'the player re-issues.' This is a genuine doc/code contradiction: the same doc claims 'the rejection is the player learning ah, that tile was full' (movement-cost.md ~line 166) but the code path generates no player-facing signal. Two fair mitigations keep it at medium rather than higher: (1) the unit's Idle Activity IS visible in the owner's view (UnitDto.Activity), so it isn't literally 'zero signal' for an attentive player; (2) the doc frames the 50-cap as a panic switch 'realistic play never bumps' (movement-cost.md:149-157), so real-world frequency is low. Still a real silent-failure gap for the fog'd cost-of-ignorance case the doc itself markets.

  [LOW] (logic-hole | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=low) Boat arrivals credit road traffic on water tiles, contradicting 'roads do not apply on water'
    EVIDENCE: MoveArrivalEvent.Apply calls Road.CreditTraffic(sim.World, To, sim.Now) unconditionally on every arrival (src/Sim.Core/Movement/MoveArrivalEvent.cs:68), with no Traversal guard.   [LOW] (balance | verdict=confirmed | conf=medium | sev0=low) MIN_COST=1 floor forces a weak Manhattan heuristic, so A* degenerates toward Dijkstra at O(tilesÃ—units)
    EVIDENCE: Heuristic returns raw Manhattan tile count (Pathfinding.cs:62-63), admissible only because the cheapest possible tile is MIN_COST=1 (RoadConstants.cs:14).     IMPACT:   The MIN_COST=1 road floor (needed to keep maxed roads attractive) caps how strong the heuristic can be without losing admissibility, so you can't scale it up. It's not per-tick (committed paths plan once per move), but a burst of retasks or many simultaneous moves can spike CPU.
    FIX:      Add the per-tile unit index the doc already proposes (Dictionary<TileCoord,List<int>>) to make crowding reads O(units-on-tile).     VERIFY:   Confirmed. The O(units) scan is acknowledged in movement-cost.md (Future expansion: per-tile unit index) and MovementCost.cs:22-27. Correctly scoped as low: paths plan once per committed move (MoveIntent.cs:91-93), not per tick, so it's a burst-CPU concern, not a steady hot loop.

  [LOW] (logic-hole | verdict=partial | conf=medium | sev0=medium) Foot units still cross Water at cost 250 post-boats, so water is never a real barrier
    EVIDENCE: TerrainCostFor dispatches Foot â†’ Road.EffectiveCost â†’ Biomes.MoveCost (src/Sim.Core/Movement/MovementCost.cs:74-79), and Biomes.MoveCost(Water)=250 (Biome.cs:33), which is < Impassable.     VERIFY:   Mechanically confirmed: TerrainCostFor routes Foot to Road.EffectiveCost->Biomes.MoveCost (MovementCost.cs:74-79); Water=250 (Biome.cs:33) which is < Impassable (int.MaxValue), and Pathfinding only skips step>=Impassable (Pathfinding.cs:48), so A* will route a foot unit across water at 250/tile. Boats' design contract (boats.md:129-146) is 'cheaper/faster than any foot route' (6 vs 250), NOT 'water is impassable for foot.' 'Never a real barrier' is also overstated: 250/tile = 25x Grassland, so a wide ocean crossing ages a unit ~1 year per ~25 tiles and is impractical on foot. The kernel of merit is that narrow straits (1-3 tiles = 250-750 ticks) are cheaply foot-crossable, so boats add little value for narrow water â€” a legitimate balance note, but it's documented-intended behavior, not a hole. Downgraded to low.

  [LOW] (logic-hole | verdict=partial | conf=high | sev0=medium) Square 4-neighbour grid forbids diagonals â€” the exact Â§6.3 artifact, making the map anisotropic
    EVIDENCE: TileGrid.Neighbors yields only N/E/S/W (src/Sim.Core/World/TileGrid.cs:33-39) and Heuristic is Manhattan (src/Sim.Core/Movement/Pathfinding.cs:62-63). There is no diagonal edge. Default map is 256Ã—256 (ServerOptions.cs:10-11).
    IMPACT:   Design Â§6.3 explicitly flags the square-grid diagonal problem and Â§13 leans hex 'on the merits for a movement-centric game.' Consequence: travel time is Manhattan, so two castles the same Euclidean distance apart can differ in travel time by up to ~41% depending on whether the line between them is axis-aligned or diagonal (a diagonal cross-map trip is 512 tiles vs ~362 Euclidean). Distance â€” the game's primary constraint â€” is therefore direction-dependent in a way players can't intuit. A* itself is correct for this topology; the issue is the topology choice contradicts the stated design lean and creates geometry artifacts.
    FIX:      This is the open hex-vs-square decision (Â§13) surfacing in code. If staying square, consider 8-neighbour with âˆš2-scaled diagonal cost (use Chebyshev/octile heuristic to stay admissible) so diagonal travel isn't penalized 2x. If movement legibility matters as much as the design says, migrate to hex (axial coords; neighbour set and heuristic change, A* core unchanged).
    VERIFY:   Facts confirmed: TileGrid.Neighbors yields only N/E/S/W (TileGrid.cs:33-39); Pathfinding.Heuristic is Manhattan (Pathfinding.cs:62-63); default map 256x256 (ServerOptions.cs:10-11); A* is correct for this topology and Manhattan travel is direction-dependent. But this is not a logic hole or contradiction â€” it's an explicitly OPEN design decision the doc permits resolving to square. persistent-rts-design.md:95 ('hex or square â€” see Â§13'), Â§6.3 line 227-230 discusses the diagonal tradeoff, and Â§13 line 494-500 says 'Leaning hex on the merits â€¦ but square is the [simpler default]' and calls it an 'open decision' (also line 551 'Pick hex vs. square'). So square is a doc-sanctioned choice, not a broken promise. The 41% anisotropy is a real property worth a design note, but framing it as a medium-severity 'logic hole' overstates a deliberate, permitted topology choice with no functional defect. Downgraded to low.

  [LOW] (balance | verdict=partial | conf=medium | sev0=medium) Per-tile move costs vs TicksPerYear make foot journeys consume years of a unit's life
    EVIDENCE: Foot Grassland = 10 ticks/tile (Biome.cs:24); other biomes 25/30/40/45; Water 250.     IMPACT:   Movement costs, road decay (DECAY_PERIOD=100), food consumption, and population aging all share one unitless tick currency, so their RATIOS are fixed regardless of wall-clock tps. Default tps=20 (ServerOptions.cs:8) gives tile=0.5s, far from Â§6.4's 'two hours each' (design line 210). All correct. But the 'problem' framing is overstated: every constant involved is explicitly a tunable placeholder (MovementConstants.cs:4 'Numbers are placeholders â€” tune in play'; PopulationConfig comment 'keeps tests tractable'), and the aging-per-distance ratio is set by TWO independent dials (TicksPerYear vs biome cost) that can be retuned to any ratio, while wall-clock feel is a third independent dial (tps). So 'moves-last-hours and lives-last-decades cannot both be true' is refuted â€” they are separately tunable, not coupled. This is legitimate cross-system tuning feedback, not a code defect. Side note found while verifying: PopulationConfig's XML comment says 'Default 100' but the ctor default is 250 (PopulationConfig.cs:8 vs 35) â€” a stale comment worth fixing. Downgraded to low.

  [LOW] (balance | verdict=partial | conf=high | sev0=high) Crowding cost plateaus at 16 units while the cap is 50 â€” the anti-deathball lever is disabled
    EVIDENCE: BandedCrowdingCost (src/Sim.Core/Movement/MovementConstants.cs:23-29): 1-3â†’+0, 4-7â†’+10, 8-15â†’+25, 16+â†’+50 (flat). MaxUnitsPerTile=50 (line 13).     IMPACT:   Design Â§7 (lines ~380-385) names 'per-tile occupancy cap' as the explicit lever against fast-relocating deathballs, and the crowding doc sells 'armies are slow via source crowding'. But because the penalty caps at 16 and the hard cap is a panic-switch 50 (the doc itself admits 'units pile on tiles in the dozens by design'), the rational play is to consolidate into one ~49-unit stack: it is no slower per tile than a 16-stack, arrives atomically (GroupArrivalEvent moves all members in one event, perfectly coherent, no straggling), and triggers combat as a single block. The mechanic actively rewards the deathball it was meant to discourage.
    FIX:      Either add escalating bands above 16 (e.g. 16-24â†’+50, 25-35â†’+90, 36-49â†’+150) so large stacks pay super-linearly, or lower MaxUnitsPerTile toward a real gameplay value (8-16) and treat the current 50 as the separate panic ceiling. The doc's 'future expansion: per-unit-kind crowding weights' is orthogonal; the immediate fix is curve shape vs. cap.
    VERIFY:   The arithmetic is correct and pinned by tests: BandedCrowdingCost (MovementConstants.cs:23-29) returns +50 flat for 16+, asserted for both 16 and 1000 in MovementCrowdingTests.BandedCrowdingCost_HitsExpectedTiers; MaxUnitsPerTile=50 (line 13); ExecutionCost adds max(source,dest) band once (MovementCost.cs:131-133). So units 17-49 added to a stack carry zero marginal movement cost â€” true. BUT the central interpretation is refuted by the design. And crowding is not 'disabled': a 16-49 stack still pays +50 on Grassland (60 vs 10 ticks/tile = 6x slower than a solo unit), so the doc's stated 'armies are slow via source crowding' goal is met. The zero-marginal-cost-above-16 plateau is a legitimate minor tuning note, not a high-severity broken/rewarded-deathball loop â€” the design never relied on this band to break stacks. Severity overstated; downgraded to low.

---------------------------------------------------------------------

##### Roads (condition/traffic/decay/emergence)  [key=roads]
SUMMARY: The road module is architecturally clean â€” lazy decay with carry-remainder, a strict pure-read wall, per-tile condition that makes junctions free, and diminishing-returns gain â€” and it is well-tested. But the balance numbers do NOT deliver the design's intended feel. The single biggest issue is that the mutation path (CreditTraffic) drops the decay remainder by resetting LastDecayTick=now, which fully suppresses decay for any road touched more often than every 100 ticks; combined with per-member group crediting and the integer cost dead-zone, roads form too easily, max too fast for groups, and almost never decay while in any use â€” while a maxed road on hard terrain (mountain/water) is nearly worthless. Several Â§8 design promises (no dead zones, slower-decay-at-higher-condition, one-citizen-can't-make-a-road) are not actually met by the code.
STRENGTHS:
  + Lazy decay with carry-remainder (in CatchUpDecay/ConditionAt) is genuinely elegant and observation-independent :: Road.cs:62-67 and 120-136 store rate + last-touched-tick and advance by completed boundaries only, carrying the remainder. The headline tests (RoadDecayTests CatchUpDecay_IsObservationIndependent / TickByTickMatchesSingleJump) prove touching a tile once vs fifty times along the way yields identical state â€” exactly the integer-exact derived-state pattern architecture Â§2.5 prescribes (the one flaw is that CreditTraffic doesn't honor it; see decay-clock-reset finding).
  + Strict pure-read wall on cost/condition queries :: EffectiveCost and ConditionAt never mutate (Road.cs:38-68), so pathfinding and views can query road-aware costs arbitrarily without injecting state drift. Enforced by 100x no-mutation hash tests at both the unit level (RoadCostTests) and the integration level (RoadPathfindingTests.Pathfinding_IsPureRead_NoRoadMutation). This is the single most important determinism property for path queries and it is well guarded.
  + Per-tile condition makes junctions and crossings free :: Condition lives per TileCoord (RoadState), so a crossroads tile simply accumulates combined traffic from every direction into one value with zero special-case junction code. The design's 'per segment' language is satisfied by tiles being the segments â€” much less code and no junction edge cases.
  + Diminishing-returns gain makes the absolute cap aspirational :: rawGain = BASE_GAIN*headroom/CONDITION_MAX (Road.cs:95) means spaced traffic asymptotes below 1000 (equilibrium c â‰ˆ 1000 - gap/5), so the very top reduction (8 / full stone) is essentially unreachable via normal equilibrium traffic and requires relentless feeding â€” correctly delivering the Â§8.4 'stone is prestigious' feel for solo traffic (the group-stamp path is the exception).
  + Single deterministic mutation point + clean fencing :: Road state changes only through CreditTraffic, called solely from arrival events (MoveArrivalEvent.cs:68, GroupArrivalEvent.cs:62), and stale/fenced arrivals don't credit (FencedArrivalEvent_DoesNotCreditRoad test). Same-tick stacking is decay-then-gain ordered so the second arrival sees the first's gain (RoadTrafficTests/RoadEmergenceTests cover it). The determinism story is tight and twin-run/snapshot tested.
FINDINGS (kept=8, refuted/dropped=0):

  [MEDIUM] (balance | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=medium) Integer cost reduction has a dead zone (condition 0â€“124 = zero speedup) and only 8 discrete speed steps â€” breaks Â§8.4 'no dead zones / responsive early formation'
    EVIDENCE: Road.cs:43: reduction = condition * MAX_REDUCTION / CONDITION_MAX = condition*8/1000 (integer). reduction only reaches 1 at condition 125, 2 at 250, ... 8 at 1000 â€” eight steps total. A first traversal yields condition 50 (BASE_GAIN, RoadConstants.cs:26) â†’ reduction 50*8/1000 = 0 â†’ NO speed benefit. ~3 solo passes (condition 150) are needed before the first +1 speed. Design Â§8.4 (persistent-rts-design.md:317-318): 'Speed reads off condition smoothly ... so every bit of traffic gives felt return â€” no dead zones' and Â§8.4:313 'early dirt-track formation feels responsive.'
    IMPACT:   The first one or two traversals of a fresh route produce literally zero speed change, and a lightly-used road can sit at condition 124 forever giving no benefit. With only 8 distinct speed levels across the entire condition range, speed is coarse/steppy rather than the promised smooth readout. Early road formation feels dead, not responsive â€” the opposite of the stated design feel.
    FIX:      Decouple cost resolution from biome integers: compute hop cost in finer units (e.g. multiply terrain by 10 internally, or carry a fixed-point reduction) so low condition gives a small but nonzero benefit and the curve has many steps. Alternatively make reduction a fraction of biome cost. Core-touching (Road.cs:43 / RoadConstants).
    VERIFY:   CONFIRMED. Road.cs:43 reduction = condition*MAX_REDUCTION/CONDITION_MAX = condition*8/1000 (integer). Verified: reduction stays 0 for condition 0-124 (124*8/1000=0), reaches 1 at 125, and only 8 distinct steps exist to condition 1000. A first traversal gives condition = BASE_GAIN = 50 (RoadConstants.cs:26), so reduction = 50*8/1000 = 0 â€” literally zero speed benefit. Tracing the diminishing-returns build, condition is 50 -> 97 -> 142 over three solo passes, so the first +1 speed arrives only around the 3rd pass (cond ~142 >= 125). This contradicts design Â§8.4:317-318 ('Speed reads off condition smoothly... no dead zones') and Â§8.4:313 ('early dirt-track formation feels responsive'). One mitigating nuance: the steppiness is partly INHERENT to integer-tick movement â€” grassland cost 10 with max reduction 8 can only express 9 distinct costs, so a truly 'smooth' curve is impossible by construction, and the design itself acknowledges visuals snap (Â§8.4:319). But the dead zone specifically (first ~2 traversals and any road that idles at condition <=124 give exactly zero benefit) is a real, defensible gap against the stated 'responsive early formation' feel. Confirmed at medium.

  [MEDIUM] (balance | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=medium) Groups credit traffic once PER MEMBER per hop â€” a big group stamps a near-stone road in a single pass, bypassing 'sustained use'
    EVIDENCE: GroupArrivalEvent.cs:57-64 loops 'foreach (group.Members)' and calls Road.CreditTraffic(world, To, sim.Now) once per member, all at the same tick. Gain per credit â‰ˆ 0.05*(1000-cond) (Road.cs:95, BASE_GAIN=50, CONDITION_MAX=1000), so k same-tick credits drive condition to ~1000*(1-0.95^k): 10 members â†’ ~400 (reduction 3, Grassland 10â†’7), 20 members â†’ ~640 (reduction 5, 10â†’5), 50 members (hard cap) â†’ ~920 (reduction 7, near-stone). There is zero decay between same-tick credits, so the whole stamp lands in ONE hop. Design Â§8.4 (persistent-rts-design.md:311-316): 'the stone road is prestigious â€” only a sustained, defended artery ever reaches and holds it.'
    IMPACT:   A single march of a large army or caravan paves a strong road across every tile it touches in one pass â€” and because roads are public (Â§8.6) and decay is suppressed for anything re-touched (see decay-clock-reset finding), that highway then persists and benefits the attacker's follow-up waves. This collapses the 'sustained over time' intent into 'bring enough units once,' and turns doomstacks into self-paving siege columns. It is flagged as intentional in the code comment (GroupArrivalEvent.cs:6-8) but the gameplay consequence is at odds with the design's prestige/sustained-artery framing.
    FIX:      Either credit a group hop once (not per member), or scale a single group credit sub-linearly with member count (e.g. credit proportional to sqrt(members) or cap per-hop gain), so group size still helps but a single pass can't reach stone. Core-touching (GroupArrivalEvent.cs / a shared gain helper in Road.cs).
    VERIFY:   CONFIRMED. GroupArrivalEvent.cs:57-64 loops every member and calls Road.CreditTraffic(world, To, sim.Now) at the same tick; CatchUpDecay is a no-op for same-now so there is zero decay between the burst credits. The diminishing-returns recurrence headroom_{k}=1000*0.95^k (rawGain=BASE_GAIN*headroom/CONDITION_MAX=0.05*headroom, Road.cs:95) gives condition ~401/641/923 for 10/20/50 members â€” matching the finding's ~400/~640/~920, and MaxUnitsPerTile=50 is confirmed (MovementConstants.cs:13), so 50 members stamps reduction 7 (cond 923 -> 923*8/1000=7, grassland 10->3, near-stone) in ONE pass. The per-member crediting is deliberate (GroupArrivalEvent.cs:5-8) but the one-tick stamp genuinely collapses the design's 'sustained use over time / prestigious stone artery' framing (Â§8.2, Â§8.4:313-316) into 'bring enough units once.' Even common 10-20 unit armies get reduction 3-5 in a single pass, and a stamped cond-920 road takes ~4500 ticks to even drop one reduction band, so it persists. Partial mitigations exist (large groups pay source-crowding cost, roads are public so the defender also benefits on counterattack) but none prevent the one-pass stamp. Real medium balance interaction.

  [MEDIUM] (balance | verdict=confirmed | conf=medium | sev0=medium) Flat additive MAX_REDUCTION makes roads dominant on cheap terrain and nearly useless on hard terrain (mountain/water)
    EVIDENCE: Road.cs:43-45 subtracts a flat reduction (max 8, RoadConstants.cs:20) from biome cost (Biome.cs:24-33). Maxed-road speedups: Grassland 10â†’2 (5x faster, -80%); Hills 25â†’17 (-32%); Forest 30â†’22 (-27%); Desert 40â†’32 (-20%); Mountain 45â†’37 (-18%); Water 250â†’242 (-3%). The flat additive caps absolute savings at 8 ticks regardless of biome, so the harder the terrain the smaller the fraction saved.
    IMPACT:   Roads are effectively a grassland-only feature. Building and maintaining a road through a mountain pass or a forest â€” exactly where a hauler most wants relief â€” buys almost nothing (18% / 27%), while flat grassland gets a 5x boost. This inverts the intuition that a paved hard-terrain route is the valuable infrastructure, and means the 'artery through difficult terrain' fantasy of Â§8 never materializes; instead optimal play always routes around hard terrain on grassland roads.
    FIX:      Make reduction scale with biome cost (e.g. a percentage reduction, capped) so a maxed mountain/forest road delivers a meaningful, comparable relative speedup. If grassland-dominance is intentional (roads = grassland arteries that route around hard terrain), document that and accept hard-terrain roads as deliberately weak. Core-touching (Road.cs / RoadConstants).
    VERIFY:   Mechanical claim CONFIRMED. Road.cs:43-45 subtracts a flat reduction (max MAX_REDUCTION=8) from biome cost; foot biome costs come from Biomes.MoveCost via TileGrid.TerrainCost (verified). Maxed-road savings: Grassland 10->2 (-80%), Hills 25->17 (-32%), Forest 30->22 (-27%), Desert 40->32 (-20%), Mountain 45->37 (-18%), Water 250->242 (-3%) â€” every percentage checks out, and the flat additive does cap absolute savings at 8 ticks so harder terrain saves a smaller fraction. So roads are mechanically grassland-dominant and nearly inert on mountain/water. The one caveat keeping this from 'fully confirmed as a doc-contradiction': design Â§8 never explicitly promises terrain-proportional benefit, so 'inverts intuition / the artery-through-difficult-terrain fantasy of Â§8 never materializes' is the auditor's interpretation rather than a stated design promise the code breaks. As a pure balance observation, though, it is solid and defensible â€” heavy investment in a mountain/forest road (exactly where a hauler most wants relief) buys 18-27%, steering all optimal routing onto grassland roads. Confirmed at medium as a balance issue; the design-doc-contradiction framing is interpretive, not literal.

  [LOW] (logic-hole | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=low) Boats stamp road condition on water tiles, where roads do nothing and only bloat the road set
    EVIDENCE: MoveArrivalEvent.cs:68 calls Road.CreditTraffic unconditionally on every arrival. Boats are Traversal.Water units moving via MoveIntent (MoveIntent.cs:23-27 confirms 'boats themselves use MoveIntent for water travel'), so each water hop fires MoveArrivalEvent â†’ CreditTraffic on a Water tile. But TerrainCostFor (MovementCost.cs:74-79) routes Water through BoatMovementCost, which ignores road condition entirely. The accumulated water-tile road state does nothing for boats and only a negligible 250â†’242 (Biome.cs:33, max reduction 8) discount for the rare foot crossing.
    IMPACT:   Semantically nonsensical ('the forest reclaims it' doesn't apply to open water) and it grows world.Roads with entries that provide no boat benefit, enlarging snapshots and the live dictionary. Harmless to determinism but wasteful and confusing for anyone inspecting road state on a water-heavy map.
    FIX:      Gate the credit on land traversal: skip Road.CreditTraffic when unit.Traversal == Traversal.Water (or when the destination biome is Water). Cheap, core-touching (MoveArrivalEvent.cs / GroupArrivalEvent.cs).
    VERIFY:   CONFIRMED. There is exactly one unit-arrival event class (MoveArrivalEvent); boats are Traversal.Water units (BoatProductionTickEvent.cs:56) that travel via MoveIntent (MoveIntent.cs:26-27) and thus fire MoveArrivalEvent, whose line 68 Road.CreditTraffic(sim.World, To, sim.Now) is unconditional â€” no biome/traversal guard. So every boat water-hop stamps RoadState on a Water tile. Meanwhile MovementCost.TerrainCostFor (MovementCost.cs:74-79) routes Water through BoatMovementCost (WaterCost=6), which ignores road condition entirely, so that accumulated state does nothing for boats â€” it only yields a negligible 250->242 discount for the rare foot crossing (foot water cost 250 via Biomes.MoveCost). Net effect is semantic noise plus dictionary/snapshot bloat on water-heavy maps. Determinism-safe and genuinely low impact, exactly as rated. Confirmed at low (one minor caveat: it is not 100% dead â€” it marginally aids foot water-crossings â€” but that is a rounding error).

  [LOW] (missing-feature | verdict=confirmed | conf=medium | sev0=low) No way to actively deny/tear up a road; decay is the only removal and any traffic (incl. enemy) defeats it
    EVIDENCE: Road condition is global, owner-agnostic state; the only removal is decay (Road.cs:118,129-132). CreditTraffic is owner-agnostic, so an enemy actively using your road keeps crediting it (MoveArrivalEvent.cs:68), and with slow decay + the decay-clock-reset, a road the enemy keeps walking never decays. Design Â§8.6 (persistent-rts-design.md:332-339) leans entirely on walls/gates at chokepoints as the counterplay; there is no sabotage/destroy-road action.
    IMPACT:   You cannot starve a road the enemy is actively raiding along â€” stopping your own traffic doesn't help if theirs continues. This is arguably by design (gates are the answer), but it means once a public artery exists near a contested border it is effectively permanent infrastructure for whoever controls the traffic, with no in-sim lever to remove it short of building a gate.
    FIX:      Confirm gates are the intended/only counter and document it; or consider an optional 'tear up road' citizen action (labor cost) for scorched-earth play. Low priority; not core-touching unless a new action is added.
    VERIFY:   CONFIRMED as a missing-feature observation, correctly self-flagged as low / largely intended. RoadState carries no owner; CreditTraffic (Road.cs:83) and ConditionAt (Road.cs:54) are owner-agnostic, and the only removal path is decay (Road.cs:118,129-132). I confirmed there is no sabotage/destroy-road intent anywhere in Roads/ â€” an enemy walking your road keeps crediting it (MoveArrivalEvent.cs:68 owner-agnostic). The 'a road the enemy keeps walking NEVER decays' clause leans on the decay-reset finding; absent that bug it would still BARELY decay (gain >> decay), so the practical conclusion (you cannot starve a road the enemy actively uses) holds regardless. This is explicitly by design: Â§8.6:336-339 states roads are public and names walls/gates at chokepoints as THE counterplay, with no destroy action contemplated â€” which the finding itself acknowledges. So it is a legitimate, accurately-described missing-feature note at low severity, heavily mitigated by being intentional. Confirmed, low.

  [LOW] (balance | verdict=partial | conf=high | sev0=medium) 'One citizen can't make a road' (Â§8.3) is false: a single unit revisiting a tile builds and maxes it alone
    EVIDENCE: Design Â§8.3 (persistent-rts-design.md:306): 'One citizen can't make a road â€” a lone walker's trickle is erased by decay.' But the break-even is per-tile traffic FREQUENCY, not distinct-citizen count. Gain per credit ~50 (Road.cs:95), decay 1/100 ticks (RoadConstants.cs:38-39). A single unit pacing a 2-tile loop on Grassland (10 ticks/tile) re-credits each tile every ~20 ticks; with the decay-clock-reset (Road.cs:105) any gap <100 ticks means ZERO decay, so both tiles climb monotonically to 1000/stone with one citizen. Even with correct carry decay, ~5 credits/100ticks vs 1 decay/100ticks still builds the road.
    IMPACT:   The literal design statement is contradicted: a lone dedicated unit is a free road-paver (its only cost is its own time, since it isn't hauling or fighting). The SPIRIT â€” that a single PASS can't make a road â€” does hold (SingleTraversal_LongSilence test). So this is a framing/balance gap rather than a crash, but worth surfacing because it interacts with the decay-reset and group-stamp findings to make roads very cheap to create and nearly impossible to lose.
    FIX:      Accept it (sustained use = sustained use, even by one unit) but fix the Â§8.3 wording; OR if a real multi-citizen threshold is wanted, that requires the decay rate to actually outpace single-unit revisit cadence â€” which first needs the decay-clock-reset fixed so decay isn't suppressed below 100-tick gaps. Tie to decay-clock-reset-suppresses-decay.
    VERIFY:   Real but a framing nuance, overstated at medium. The literal sentence Â§8.3:306 ('One citizen can't make a road') is mechanically contradictable: gain ~50/credit (Road.cs:95) vs decay 1/100 ticks means one unit pacing a short loop re-credits each tile far faster than decay erodes it, so the road climbs â€” I confirmed it builds toward cap even WITHOUT relying on the decay-reset bug (near cap, ~5 credits/100ticks at floor gain 1 still nets positive vs 1 decay/100ticks). BUT the design's SPIRIT clearly holds and is tested: a single PASS followed by silence decays away with no lasting road (SingleTraversal_LongSilence_Decays_NoLastingRoad, RoadEmergenceTests.cs:15-37; also RoadTrafficTests.cs:76-95). The design word 'trickle' denotes incidental/occasional traffic, not a unit DEDICATED to nothing but pacing â€” which carries a real opportunity cost (that unit isn't hauling, working, or fighting). So this is a literal-wording vs. spirit gap, and arguably a feature (you may spend a citizen's time to bootstrap infrastructure), not a balance break. The 'climbs to stone' strength partly leans on the separate decay-reset finding. Partial, low.

  [LOW] (logic-hole | verdict=partial | conf=high | sev0=high) CreditTraffic resets LastDecayTick=now (drops carry remainder) â†’ traffic faster than every 100 ticks never decays at all
    EVIDENCE: Road.cs:85 CreditTraffic calls CatchUpDecay; at a sub-period gap CatchUpDecay returns WITHOUT advancing LastDecayTick (Road.cs:123-124, 'if (periods <= 0) return; // leave remainder banked'). Then Road.cs:104-105 unconditionally does 'road.Condition = newCondition; road.LastDecayTick = now;'. So every credit overwrites LastDecayTick with `now`, throwing away the sub-period remainder. DECAY_PERIOD=100 (RoadConstants.cs:39). Trace, road fed every 50 ticks from scratch: t=0 cond=50 LDT=0; t=50 elapsed=50â†’periods=0â†’no decay, gain, LDT:=50 (50 ticks dropped); t=100 elapsed=50â†’0â†’LDT:=100; ... decay NEVER triggers, road climbs monotonically to 1000 and stays. By t=1000 the carry model would have applied ~10 decay; the code applies 0. This directly contradicts the carry-remainder invariant the SAME file's header sells (Road.cs:19-27) and architecture.md:92-100 ('If lastTick were set to now and the remainder dropped, the result would depend on how often you observed'). It is NOT a determinism break (CreditTraffic only fires from deterministic arrival events; ConditionAt/EffectiveCost never reset), but the documented 'abandoned maxed road takes 100,000 ticks to vanish' (RoadConstants.cs:35) only holds for FULLY abandoned roads â€” any road touched even once per <100 ticks is effectively immortal, and non-100-aligned gaps systematically under-count decay.
    IMPACT:   This is balance-defining. The whole emergence economy assumes 'traffic feeds, time drains' as competing continuous rates with a smooth break-even (Â§8.3). In reality decay is a step function: gap < 100 ticks â†’ zero decay; gap >= 100 â†’ partial, remainder-forgiven decay. Maintained roads therefore decay far slower than the nominal 1/100, so 'keep it fed' is trivially satisfied by light use and roads almost never slide back while in any use. It also makes the nominal decay constant misleading to anyone tuning balance, and the module internally contradicts itself (CatchUpDecay/ConditionAt carry the remainder; CreditTraffic discards it). The CatchUpDecay_IsObservationIndependent test passes only because it exercises CatchUpDecay in isolation, never interleaved with CreditTraffic's reset.
    FIX:      Decide on ONE decay model and make all three paths agree. If decay is meant to be true wall-time rate (as the carry comments and architecture Â§2.5 say): in CreditTraffic advance LastDecayTick by completed periods only (mirror CatchUpDecay) instead of '= now'. If the intent is an 'abandonment timer' that resets on every touch, then keep the reset but rewrite the Road.cs:19-27 carry comments and the RoadConstants decay comment to state that, and re-tune the rate knowing maintained roads never decay. Core-touching (Sim.Core/Roads/Road.cs).
    VERIFY:   Mechanism CONFIRMED exactly as described. Road.cs:104-105 unconditionally sets road.LastDecayTick = now after CatchUpDecay. When a road is below cap and credited at gaps < DECAY_PERIOD (100), CatchUpDecay returns at line 124 ('periods<=0... leave remainder banked') without advancing LastDecayTick, then line 105 overwrites it to now â€” so elapsed never accumulates to a full period and decay never fires during the build-up phase. This directly contradicts the carry invariant the same file documents (Road.cs:15-27) and architecture.md:92-100. I confirmed the observation-independence tests (RoadDecayTests.cs:25-74) only call CatchUpDecay in isolation, never through CreditTraffic, so nothing covers the reset; removing line 105 would not break any existing test. HOWEVER the 'high / balance-defining' rating is overstated for two reasons. (1) Determinism-safe â€” the finding itself concedes this; CreditTraffic fires only from deterministic arrival events and pure reads (ConditionAt/EffectiveCost) carry the remainder correctly. (2) Negligible balance impact at the actual constants: decay is 1 per 100 ticks while a single credit gains up to BASE_GAIN=50 (Road.cs:95, RoadConstants.cs:26,38). The correct carry model would apply only ~10 condition of decay per 1000 ticks â€” a sub-1-tick speed difference on grassland (reduction = cond*8/1000). So 'roads are immortal while in use' is already approximately true under the CORRECT model; the bug just turns negligible decay into exactly zero. Also note: at cap the headroom<=0 early-return (Road.cs:93) fires BEFORE line 105, so maxed roads are NOT clock-reset and do decay+refill. The real residual concern is a latent tuning hazard â€” if a balance pass raised DECAY_PER_PERIOD to make in-use roads decay meaningfully, this reset would silently defeat it. Real internal-contradiction bug worth fixing, but low gameplay severity, not high.

  [LOW] (logic-hole | verdict=partial | conf=high | sev0=medium) Â§8.5 'slower at higher condition (stone holds up longer than dirt)' is specified but the code uses condition-independent constant-rate decay
    EVIDENCE: Design Â§8.5 (persistent-rts-design.md:326-328): 'Slower at higher condition ("stone holds up longer than dirt"): reaching the top tier buys not just speed but resilience â€” a few quiet days forgiven where a dirt track wouldn't be.' Code: DECAY_PER_PERIOD=1, DECAY_PERIOD=100 applied flat in Road.cs:65 and Road.cs:126 regardless of condition; RoadConstants.cs:36-38 comment explicitly rejects condition-dependent decay as 'the coupled-interval trap.' Constant rate gives absolute resilience only via more-to-burn (stone 1000â†’100,000 ticks vs dirt 125â†’12,500 ticks), but the per-condition RATE is identical.
    IMPACT:   A stone road that loses traffic slides through the entire mid-range at the same 1/100 rate as a dirt track â€” it does not 'hold up longer' in the decelerating sense the design asks for. The 'coupled-interval trap' justification is weak here: the codebase already implements deterministic condition-dependent rate changes elsewhere (biome-degradation Â§step-penalty, architecture.md:114-119 transition-anchor pattern), so a band-scaled decay rate is achievable without nondeterminism. As written, the design promise is half-kept (absolute durability) and half-broken (rate shape).
    FIX:      Either implement a condition-banded decay rate using the catch-up-at-transition pattern (architecture Â§2.5), giving high-condition roads a genuinely slower per-tick rate, or amend Â§8.5 to state resilience comes from total-condition-to-burn under a constant rate, not a slower rate. Core-touching if implemented (Road.cs / RoadConstants).
    VERIFY:   Facts CONFIRMED but framed too strongly as a broken promise. Design Â§8.5:326-328 does ask for 'slower at higher condition (stone holds up longer than dirt),' and the code uses a flat condition-independent rate (DECAY_PER_PERIOD=1/DECAY_PERIOD=100, Road.cs:65,126), giving only absolute durability (stone 1000->100,000 ticks vs dirt 125->12,500) and not a decelerating rate. The finding is also right that the 'coupled-interval trap' rejection (RoadConstants.cs:36-38) is weak as a blanket excuse: architecture.md:110 explicitly sanctions a BAND-STEPPED rate ('catch up to the next band at the current flat rate; switch rate at the boundary') and BiomeDegradation (architecture.md:117-119) already does deterministic band-stepped value changes â€” so a band-scaled decay rate is achievable without nondeterminism. HOWEVER this is mitigated by explicit deferral, not a kept-then-broken promise: Â§8.5 is titled 'Decay tempo (decided DIRECTION; exact rate still OPEN)' (line 322) and RoadConstants.cs:3-5 says 'Numbers are placeholders â€” tune during balance, lock the *shape* now.' Implementing flat constant rate as a first-cut placeholder while the rate shape remains an open design item is consistent with that. Real gap, accurately analyzed, but it is a deliberately-open future refinement rather than a code-breaks-the-spec defect. Partial, low.

---------------------------------------------------------------------

##### Logistics & Economy (haul/produce/extract/build)  [key=logistics-economy]
SUMMARY: The produce->haul->build->produce loop genuinely closes (EndToEndLoopTests proves it) and the "everything physical" principle is honored in the core path â€” no teleport holes for builds or hauls, and resource containers clamp correctly so no negative/duplicate stock in the happy path. The event-driven back-pressure/re-arm and epoch-fencing patterns are clean and deterministic. However, the subsystem has one serious state-corruption bug (ghost workers never pruned on death), a guaranteed resource-loss path (cargo overwrite + no unload), a live multiplayer authorization hole (no ownership check lets a player remotely sabotage an enemy economy), and an economy that structurally stalls while the player is offline â€” which directly contradicts the game's async "geological time" premise. Cargo capacity also dead-ends because births fix it at 1 and no in-game mechanism raises it.
STRENGTHS:
  + Epoch-fencing makes retasking deterministic and safe :: Unit.AssignmentEpoch (Unit.cs:71) is captured at schedule time and re-checked when HaulPickup/HaulDeposit/MoveArrival fire (HaulPickupEvent.cs:47, HaulDepositEvent.cs:42, MoveArrivalEvent.cs:33). A unit retasked mid-haul cleanly no-ops its stale events with no mutation â€” a single, generalized pattern that closes a whole class of double-apply/order bugs and survives snapshot/replay.
  + Event-driven back-pressure with no polling :: Production stops rescheduling when the buffer fills or workers leave and re-arms only via ArmIfDormant on a haul pickup or worker assignment (ProductionTickEvent.cs:101-117, Structure.cs:153-169). The dormant-when-unsatisfiable / re-arm-when-satisfiable rule is reused identically for construction pause/resume â€” elegant and centralized.
  + Resource containers cannot go negative or duplicate in the core path :: StorageStructure.Deposit/Withdraw clamp to FreeSpace/Holdings (Structure.cs:37-58), ConstructionSite.Deposit clamps to Outstanding (Structure.cs:252-261), and extractor pickup clamps to min(capacity, buffer) (HaulPickupEvent.cs:88). Over-delivery can't inflate stock and withdraws can't underflow.
  + Capture economy actually closes the Â§7.3 loop :: A laden unit's cargo drops to world.GroundResources on death (CombatRules.cs:108-120) and HaulIntent/HaulPickup treat a ground pile as a valid haul source (HaulIntent.cs:51-58, HaulPickupEvent.cs:79-118). Goods are captured-not-destroyed and the raider must physically haul them away, exactly as the design intends.
  + Everything-physical genuinely holds for builds and hauls :: Construction requires materials hauled onto the build tile and builders physically present (ConstructionSite.ConditionsMet, Structure.cs:279-280; deposits only via HaulDepositEvent), with no castle-as-magic-source path â€” and EndToEndLoopTests proves the produce->haul->build->produce loop closes through every cross-system hook. The keystone principle is respected by the actual code, not just the doc.
FINDINGS (kept=8, refuted/dropped=0):

  [HIGH] (logic-hole | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=high) Economy intents don't check the issuing player â€” remote sabotage of an enemy's economy with zero presence
    EVIDENCE: Intent.PlayerId exists but its own comment says it is only '(eventually) used by validation when intents become player-scoped (e.g. AssignWorkers must target a structure owned by the issuing player)' (src/Sim.Core/Intents/Intent.cs:9-12). None of UnassignWorkersIntent, AssignWorkersIntent, AssignBuildersIntent, or HaulIntent compare PlayerId to the OwnerId of the structures/units involved. UnassignWorkersIntent only checks the worker is in extractor.Workers, alive, Working, and assigned to the tile (src/Sim.Core/Logistics/UnassignWorkersIntent.cs:31-40) â€” none of which require the issuer to own anything or be present. Units and Structures both carry OwnerId (Unit.cs:55, Structure.cs:11) and multi-faction worlds + combat are live (MultiFactionGenesisTests, CombatRules).
    IMPACT:   A player can submit UnassignWorkersIntent(enemyExtractorTile, [enemyWorkerIds]) and instantly empty an enemy's camp from across the map with no unit present â€” pure remote griefing. Similarly a player could direct their own units standing on an enemy structure, or haul out of an enemy Castle, without authorization checks. This breaks the central 'knowledge comes from presence' principle and the async-fairness contract (you can be sabotaged while asleep with no telegraph).
    FIX:      core-touching: gate each economy intent on PlayerId == target structure/unit OwnerId (and for cross-faction interaction, require presence or a war state). This is already flagged in the Intent.PlayerId comment as intended-but-unbuilt.
    VERIFY:   Verified, and actually slightly worse than stated: the gap is unguarded not just in Sim.Core but also server-side. None of UnassignWorkersIntent/AssignWorkersIntent/AssignBuildersIntent/HaulIntent compare PlayerId to OwnerId. UnassignWorkersIntent.cs:31-40 only requires the extractor to exist, the id to be in Workers, the unit alive+Working+Assigned to the tile â€” no presence, no ownership. Intent.cs:9-12 explicitly states ownership validation is '(eventually) used... e.g. AssignWorkers must target a structure owned by the issuing player' i.e. acknowledged-but-unbuilt. The server (GameHost.SubmitEnvelopeJson, GameHost.cs:76-93) deserializes the client envelope and calls _sim.SubmitIntent directly with NO ownership/auth gate, so a client can submit UnassignWorkersIntent(enemyExtractorTile, enemyWorkerIds) and empty an enemy camp from across the map with zero presence. Directly breaks the 'knowledge comes from presence' principle and fair-while-asleep (untelegraphed remote sabotage). High confirmed.

  [HIGH] (logic-hole | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=high) Leftover/overflow cargo is silently destroyed â€” pickup overwrites cargo and there is no unload intent
    EVIDENCE: HaulPickupEvent assigns rather than adds: `hauler.CargoResource = Resource; hauler.CargoAmount = pickup;` (src/Sim.Core/Logistics/HaulPickupEvent.cs:120-121). HaulIntent.Resolve never checks that the hauler is empty before starting (src/Sim.Core/Logistics/HaulIntent.cs:32-81). Deposit overflow is intentionally left on the unit (HaulDepositEvent.cs:90; tested as a feature in HaulIntentTests.Deposit_OverflowStaysOnHauler, and MoveIntent comments 'Cargo on a Hauling unit stays with them', MoveIntent.cs:42). There is no intent in the Intents/Logistics set that deposits already-carried cargo. So once a hauler ends a trip holding leftover/overflow cargo, the only way to retask it (a new HaulIntent) overwrites that cargo at pickup and the previous amount/resource is lost.
    IMPACT:   Overflow happens routinely when depositing into a near-full Castle/Stockpile or over-hauling a construction site (Deposit caps at FreeSpace/Outstanding, Structure.cs:37-45 / 252-261). The carried remainder cannot be recovered and is destroyed the moment the player issues the next haul â€” a direct violation of conservation in a game whose whole point is that resources are physical and must be hauled.
    FIX:      Add an explicit Unload/Deposit-carried-cargo intent (deposit hauler cargo into the structure on its current tile), and make HaulPickupEvent additive only when CargoResource matches (reject the haul if the hauler already carries a different resource) instead of clobbering.
    VERIFY:   Verified. HaulPickupEvent.cs:120-121 assigns `hauler.CargoResource=Resource; hauler.CargoAmount=pickup;` â€” overwrite, not add â€” and the pre-overwrite remainder is destroyed (it is not deposited anywhere, so world conservation is violated). HaulIntent.Resolve (HaulIntent.cs:32-81) never checks CargoAmount==0 before starting. There is no unload/deposit-only intent anywhere in the intent set (confirmed by enumerating src/Sim.Core/**/*Intent.cs). Overflow-stays-on-hauler is an intended, tested behavior (HaulIntentTests.Deposit_OverflowStaysOnHauler, lines 208-235, leaves 4 wood on the unit) and is routinely reachable: over-hauling a near-complete ConstructionSite (Outstanding caps the deposit, Structure.cs:252-261) or a near-full StorageStructure (FreeSpace caps it, Structure.cs:37-45). No code path lets the player recover that remainder â€” the next HaulIntent overwrites it at pickup. Magnitude scales with capacity: a boat hauler (CargoCapacity 100, BoatConstants.cs:13) over-depositing into a near-full Castle can strand and then lose a large amount. Real conservation violation in a conservation-centric game; high stands.

  [HIGH] (logic-hole | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=high) Dead workers stay in extractor.Workers forever â€” permanent ghost slot + infinite no-op production reschedule
    EVIDENCE: Extractor.Workers is an explicit SortedSet<int> (src/Sim.Core/World/Structure.cs:120). The death pipeline CombatRules.OnUnitDeath (src/Sim.Core/Combat/CombatRules.cs:70-149) drops cargo, clears group/movement/haul anchors and removes the unit from world.Units, but never removes the id from any extractor's Workers set. ProductionTickEvent treats Workers.Count>0 as 'staffed' (src/Sim.Core/Logistics/ProductionTickEvent.cs:63), then skips ids whose unit is gone (rate += nothing, :87-95), computes extract=0, and reschedules because Workers.Count>0 && !BufferFull (:101-107). UnassignWorkersIntent cannot clean it up either: it does `if (!world.Units.TryGetValue(id, out var unit)) continue;` (src/Sim.Core/Logistics/UnassignWorkersIntent.cs:35) so a dead id is unremovable. Builders use the opposite (position+activity) recompute (ConstructionSite.BuildersPresent, Structure.cs:267-277), so they self-heal â€” the worker path is the inconsistent one.
    IMPACT:   Workers die of old age while assigned (lifespan 50-80yr at TicksPerYear=250 = 12,500-20,000 ticks; PopulationConfig.cs:35-42) or in a raid on the camp tile, so this WILL happen in a persistent game. Each ghost permanently consumes a WorkerCap slot (cap 3), and if the ghost is the camp's only 'worker' the camp looks staffed (Workers.Count=1) yet produces 0 forever while an armed ProductionTickEvent reschedules every 10 ticks indefinitely â€” a soft-locked structure the player cannot fix and a slow unbounded event leak that persists across snapshots (NextProductionTickSeq is serialized).
    FIX:      core-touching: prune the id from every extractor's Workers in CombatRules.OnUnitDeath / Population.OnUnitRemoved, OR make ProductionTickEvent drop missing-unit ids from the set as it scans (and go dormant when the live count hits 0). The cleanest fix mirrors BuildersPresent: treat Workers as advisory and reconcile against world.Units each tick.
    VERIFY:   Verified. All three death paths (combat CombatRoundEvent.cs:118, age DeathByAgeEvent.cs:45, starvation StarvationDeathEvent.cs:69) funnel through CombatRules.OnUnitDeath (CombatRules.cs:70-149), which drops cargo/clears group/movement/haul anchors and removes from world.Units but never touches any extractor.Workers set. Population.OnUnitRemoved (Population.cs:110-148) also never touches Workers. The ONLY worker-removal is MoveIntent.CleanUpAssignment (MoveIntent.cs:69) on retask. UnassignWorkersIntent.cs:35 does `if (!world.Units.TryGetValue(id, out var unit)) continue;` AFTER confirming the id is in Workers (line 34) but BEFORE the Remove (line 38), so a dead id is genuinely unremovable. ProductionTickEvent.cs:63 only goes dormant when Workers.Count==0 â€” a lone ghost keeps Count==1, so :87-95 skips the dead id (rate 0), extract=0, and :101 reschedules forever (every 10 ticks for a LumberCamp). The ghost also permanently consumes a WorkerCap slot (AssignWorkersIntent.cs:40 counts it). No test in ProductionTests.cs exercises death-while-Working. Two real harms: (a) unconditional permanent cap-slot leak (a camp whose workers die in place degrades to all-ghost and soft-locks, unfixable by the player); (b) unbounded ProductionTickEvent reschedule leak when the ghost is the sole worker. Reachable in a persistent game (lifespan 50-80yr at TicksPerYear=250). High is justified.

  [MEDIUM] (missing-feature | verdict=confirmed | conf=high | sev0=medium) No way to cancel/demolish a structure or construction site â€” misplacement permanently blocks the tile and burns materials
    EVIDENCE: The full intent set (DisembarkIntent, EmbarkIntent, war/diplomacy, group, AssignBuilders/Workers, Haul, PlaceSite, Unassign, Move, BeginBreeding, TrainUnit) contains no demolish/cancel intent. PlaceSiteIntent rejects any tile that already holds a structure (src/Sim.Core/Logistics/PlaceSiteIntent.cs:41-42), and a ConstructionSite's Delivered materials are simply discarded when BuildCompleteEvent removes the site (BuildCompleteEvent.cs:57) â€” there is no path that returns Delivered materials or removes a never-completed site.
    IMPACT:   If a player places a site in the wrong spot (or can never gather enough builders/materials), that tile is blocked forever and any wood already hauled into the site is unrecoverable. The design doc says materials are 'consumed at start' but the code holds them in Delivered indefinitely with no reclaim, so an abandoned site is a permanent dead zone and a sunk cost.
    FIX:      Add a CancelSite/Demolish intent that removes the site/structure (owner-gated) and optionally returns a fraction of Delivered materials as a ground pile on the tile, reusing the existing GroundResources capture-economy plumbing.
    VERIFY:   Verified. Enumerated all src/Sim.Core/**/*Intent.cs â€” no demolish/cancel intent exists. The only Structures.Remove in Sim.Core is BuildCompleteEvent.cs:57 (consumes the site on successful completion; Delivered materials are simply dropped, not refunded).
